'fl                                                  INTRODUCTION
1.1    Background of the Project
India has embarked on a 10-year highway transportation improvement program as a part
of the national policy to globalize its economy to increase safety and efficiency in trade
and business and also free movement of traffic National Highways comprise about 2% of
total road length in the country and yet carry over 40% of total traffic of the country.
To develop, maintain and manage the National Highways and for matters connected on
incidental there to "National Highways Authority of India (NHAI)" was constituted by an
act of Parliament, the National Highways Authorities of India Act, 1988. The first and
foremost task mandated to national Highways Authority of India (NHAI) is the
implementation of National Highways Development Projects (NHDP) comprising of
Golden Quadrilateral and North-South and East-West corridors. The proposed Allahabad
bypass project is a part of golden quadrilateral (GQ) scheme comprising of the following
stretches:
Stretch                Length (km)
Delhi - Kolkata                   1469
Calcutta - Chennai                1751
Chennai - Mumbai                  1278
Mumbai - Delhi                    1454
(GQ) Total                        5952
Among these, Delhi-Kolkata is connected by existing National Highway 2 (NH-2) The
NH-2 is one of the oldest high density corridor in India connecting the two metropoliton
cities New Delhi and Kolkata. It traverses through the states of Delhi, Uttar Pradesh,
Bihar and West Bengal. Delhi to Agra section at the Western end and Dhanbad to Kolkata
section at the Eastern End of the corridor are either already 4 laned or are in the process
of being 4 laned. The remaining about 1000 km long corridor falling between Agra and
Dhanbad is still two laned and is being considered for four laning under the proposed
World Bank NH project. This is divided into two parts i.e., the Third National Highway
Project (TNHP) and the Grand Trunk Road Improvement Project (GTRIP). The (TNHP)
aims at strengthening and four laning of 478 kms of selected stretches between Agra to
Dhanbad as per details given under Table I 1.
in association with

1.2       oPR Consultants
The detailed project report has been prepared by the Consortium consisting SNC Lavalin
International and Gherzi Eastern Ltd. for bypass construction packages ABP-2 and ABP-
3. M/s SNC Lavalin International were lead consultants. M/s Tandon Consultants (P) Ltd
were associated consultants for Ganga Bridge package, ABP-1. M/s Gherzi Eastern Ltd.,
Kolkata, has carried out the environmental and social assessments for the project The
consultants had first undertaken environnmental screening exercise to identify critical
environmental issues of the project, which were focal point of the environmental impact
assessment.
1.3      Independent Environmental Reviewers
Allahabad bypass project has been categorized as category 'A' project due to its
cumulative magnitude of social and environmental impacts. Because of this reason and as
per policy of The World Bank independent reviewers have been appointed to review the
EIA process adopted in project preparation and to consolidate EIA and SIA documents.
The independent reviewers are consortium consisting of DHV Consultants and MDP
consultants (P) Ltd., New Delhi.
The reviewers have reviewed the EIA process adopted and have prepared Independent
Environmental review report. Having identified the missing gaps in EA process adopted
the independent reviewers have consolidated EIA and SIA documents after collecting the
missing data.
1.4       Concept and need of BY-pass for Allahabad Town
Over a period of time traffic volume has increased many fold on National Highway -2
and one encounters acute traffic congestion while passing through city of Allahabad. The
reason for intensified traffic in Allahabad city, is besides NH-2, four other major roads,
namely NH-27 (connecting Rewa), SH-7 (connecting Jaunpur), SH-9 (connecting
Lucknow) and SH-47 (connecting Mirzapur) are also merging to this town. As per the
latest traffic census, the traffic volume on NH-2 on either side of Allahabad town is of the
order of 8000-10000 PCUs of fast moving vehicles, where as intensity of traffic within
the city Is of the order of 25000-28000 PCUs of fast moving vehicles. This gives a fair
idea of the extent of 'slow traffic' mixing up with 'through traffic' of NH-2 passing
through Allahabad town (Photoplate-1). Due to the increased traffic and restricted width
of carriage way operative speed in Allahabad town is of the order of 10 kmph on NH-2.
in association with



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2
In order to achieve higher operative speed for 'through traffic' in future years following
options were explored.
- Widening the existing NH-2 in city portion to four lanes;
-  Construction of elevated structure in town above the existing NH-2 carriageway;
and
- Construction of Bypass
The widening of National Highway - 2 in the town portion was not feasible because it
could have resulted in massive destruction of properties, displacement of large number of
people and R&R problems. The construction of elevated structure was rejected due to
financial and environmental considerations, as construction of elevated structure would
have also reduced sunlight to the households on either side of NH-2.
Bypass to the city was found to be the most viable option. The present alignment of
bypass was adopted for detailed DPR after careful evaluation of various alternative
alignments. The details on "Selection of present alignment are covered in the following
section.
1.5   Selection of Present Alignment (Analysis of Alternativel
Total seven alternatives alignments were considered for the bypass of NH 2 for the
Allahabad town Among these seven alternative alignments, only one alignment (ASI)
was on southern side, five (AN I through AN5), were on the northern side of Allahabad
Town and the seventh one is a14.5 km long elevated structure with four lane above the
existing National Highway within Allahabad town During the feasibility phase of the
project a separate study was under taken for the finalization of the alignment and a
separate report named, as "Selection of Alternatives" was prepared. For the selection of
best alternative the relative merits of various alternatives were assessed by a weighted
marking scheme. The weights were allotted to various factors which could influence the
choice i.e , capital cost, scope of future widening, land acquisition, user cost, impact on
environment, suitability of alignment etc. For each of these factors the various
alternatives were marked on a scale of 1 to 5. The marks were multiplied by various
weights. The sum of weighted marks provided an objective ranking of various
alternatives. Numerical values to various factors were assigned by consensus with experts
in different fields and NEAI officials The marks in two categories namely capital cost
and land acquisition were calculated numerically, but for other categories were arrived
subjectively after discussion with various experts. Based on described evaluation,
alternative 'AN-4' was found to be the most viable option This report has been submitted
to the World Bank and accepted by them. The map showing all alternative alignment is
given in Figure 1.2.
The present environmental impact assessment report covers the study of selected
alignment, AN-4

1.6       Environmental ImPact Assessment Process Adopted
Incorporation of Environmental considerations into the project planning and design have
been taken up as an integral part of the project preparation. The major steps in EA process
that have been adopted for this project are presented in the following subsections.
1.6.1     Environmental Screening
The feasibility study incorporated a social and environmental screening. In the screening
stage all environmental and social aspects of bypass were identified. The impacts or
various environmental and social aspects were also considered in project influence area.
Informal local level public consultations were held during screening to access likely
impacts on project influence area (PIA) Based on screening, EIA was focussed on
potentially significant environmental issues.
1.6.2     Environmental impactAssessment and Management Plans
The detailed designs of bypass alignment have been closely coordinated with the
preparation of Environmental Impact Assessment Study. The EIA preparation led to
identification of potential environmental hotspots and their feasible remedial measures
(including avoidance, mitigation and enhancements) that were made a part of
environmental management plan.
1.7       Structures of this EIA Report
This EIA report has been presented as per requirements of the Environmental Impact
Assessment notification of Ministry of Environment and Forests (MoEF) under the
Environment Protection Act, 1986, and World Bank Operative Directives. In the report
preparation guidelines of the Indian Road Congress (1989), Environmental guidelines for
Rail / Road / Highway projects of MoEF and World Bank Operative Directives have been
followed. Entire report has been discussed in remaining eight chapters. Brief of coverage
in each chapter is described below
Chapter 2-Project Description
In this chapter selected bypass alignment has been discussed from an environmental
perspective along with salient features such as RoW, cross section, community facilities,
etc. The traffic projections are also presented. An overview of impacts of the entire
project, mainly benefits, is given in the last section. This chapter also includes the general
features, man made features, highway design and planning, bridge design and planning,
etc.
Chapter 3 dIscusses the Policy, Legal and Administrative Framework within which
the project is set. The major stakeholder departments of the State and Central
Governments with their specific roles are described here along with the applicable Acts
and Laws. This chapter also covers the clearance requirements at various levels and their
current status.

Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for

Chapter 4 details out the Methodology adopted for the Environmental Impact
Assessment. Descriptions are provided for survey procedures, modelling methods and
environmental designs.
Chapter 5 describes the Existing Environmental Scenario in detail. The sections on
meteorological baseline, components of the physical and natural environments, cultural
properties along the corridor and quality of life have been added up to give a
comprehensive picture of the existing environment along the bypass alignment and its
area of influence.
Chapter 6 gives an overview of the Community Consultation carried out during the
project preparation stage It also provides an insight into the processes involved, its
importance to project design and methods adopted to document the entire exercise.
Chapter 7 on the Assessment of Impacts detenrines the extent of the impacts of the
project activity on the existing environment. The focus of section is on the adverse
impacts. The beneficial impacts on the environment due to the project have been detailed
in subsequent chapter under the enhancement measures. The impacts have been detailed
in the same sequence as described in Chapter 5 for ease of understanding
Chapter 8 entitled Mitigation, Avoidance and Enhancement Measures forms the basis
of the generation of coherent, comprehensive and concise Environmental Management
Plans for the project corridors In addition to the avoidance and mitigation measures for
the physical and natural environmental components, this chapter discusses various
environmental enhancements suggested for the project including the enhancement of
common property resources.
Chapter 9 reviews the existing Implementation Arrangements and suggests further
institutional strengthening for ease of implementation of the environmental component of
the project. It goes on to describe the set-up required, a reporting system and training
needs to ensure that the environmental expertise required for the effective implementation
of EA provisions is intemalised at NHAI. Report ends with annexures. An Executive
Summary has also been prepared.
1.0      Stand Alone Environmental Management Plans
Stand-alone environmental management plans have been prepared separately for all the
three construction packages The EMPs have monitoring plans; enhancement plans
environmental management costs, monitoring frequency, detail of mitigation and
responsible agency for implementation.

PROJECT DESCRIPTION
2.1     PROJECT DESCRIPTION
The total length of Allahabad bypass is 84.708 kni, divided into three construction
packages. It has been' designed as an access controlled toll road with divided 4-lane
carriageway for the present and provision of 6-lanning for future. Based on traffic
projections the design life of the project is 30 years A 70 m Right of Way (RoW) has
been proposed for the project corridor and wherever there are high embankments,
interchanges, toll plaza, rest area, etc., a 90 m RoW has been envisaged.
The three construction packages are as stated below:
ABP - I .        Ganga bridge with river training work from krm 163.267 to km
164.280 (1 03 km)
ABP-2.           From km 158.000 to km 163.267 and km 164.280 to km 198.0
(38.987 kni)
ABP - 3:         From km 198.0 to km 242.708 (44.708km)
2.1.1    Proposed Bypass Carriageway Details
Highway design is based as per relevant IRC standards and international codes of
practice The bypass will have access control with provision of parallel service roads. No
access will be allowed to slow moving vehicles on the main carriageway except at
interchanges and, toll plazas. Specifications of RoW, carriageway, service road,
shoulders, median, etc are given below:

2.1.2    Description of Area and Terrain
Alignment along NH-2 on Existing RoW
The existing NH-2 from km 158 to 160 and km 244 to 245 forms part of this bypass
package. In these stretches terratn is flat and RoW is passing through agricultural land.
The widening of these NH-2 portions to four lanning is planned as a part of bypass. In
order to accommodate the widening, land acquisition will be there. Major interchanges
are planned at start point (km 160) and end point of (km 242.0) to segregate the through
traffic from local traffic. There are three culverts in the portion between km 158 to 160
and two culverts in km 244 to 245. These culverts will be strengthened and extended in
the widening.
2-2                       /       4C
in association with                   1



Consobdated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2
Alignment of Bypass on New RoW
The bypass takes off NH-2 alignment at km 160 and traverses in an east direction about 2
km along the existing NH 2 then turns north-east through the cultivated land. It crosses
Ganga River at krm 163, and continues in the same direction upto km 167, crossing Doab
River (km 166.362). From there it heads eastward crossings SH 38 (at km 185.544) and
Lucknow - Allahabad Rail link at km 188.749. From there it continues in north-east
direction and crosses SH 9 at km 196.600 and heads towards east to cross Pratapgarh-
Allahabad Rail link at km 199.810. The alignment now takes south-east direction crossing
SH7 at km 216.816 and turns south ward at km 230 to meet NH-2 at km 242.10 and ends
at km 245 of existing NH-2.
The area through which the new alignment of bypass passes is predominantly agricultural
land. This agriculture land falls under irrigated category, due to canal network, catering to
four crops a year As the bypass alignment passes through agriculture land there will be
minimum impact on human settlements

2.3.1    Natural Features
The terrain from km 158.00 to km 242.708 along the proposed bypass route is flat with
maximum gradient of 3%. 29 streams, nullahs and drains, 3 rivers and 32 canals cutting
across the proposed bypass alignment.

2.3.3    Fencing
Fencing of chain link type is proposed on the carriageway of bypass to prevent entry of
village population, pedestrians, stray animals, slow moving traffic, etc. 1 8m high chain
link fencing will be there all along the length of bypass except at high embankment
stretches and interchanges. The length of chain link fencing in ABP-II and ABP-III is
34.75 km and 41 km respectively.
2.3.8    RoW and Land Acouusition
A 70m wide RoW in general, has been provided for the bypass. This RoW is required to
accommodate main carriageway, median, service roads and a compacted earth surface on
either side of median in between carriageway and service road Typical cross section for
four lane divided carriageway with service road, four lane divided carriageway without
service road, 6 lane divided carriageway with service road and for high embankment
(bridge approach on Ganga river) have been shown in Figures 2.2 - 2.5. Since the bypass
is going to be access controlled, grade separation structures have been proposed to be
introduced in every crossing of Railway lines and important cross roads like SH, ODR
and paved village roads. This has made the profile of bypass high at frequent locations.
Additional land take to the tune of 20 m to make the ROW 90 m wide at high
embankment locations has been proposed. This will help to introduce milder and stable
slope to the filling and avoid costly options like retaining wall or reinforced earth
structures at most of the places. At the approaches of ROBs and Ganga bridge retaining
wall/reinforced earth structures are planned Acquisition of approximately 781 ha land
will be required, out of which about 687 ha is private land and the remaining 94 ha is
Govt. land
2.3.9    Fly Overs
The bypass crosses over SH 38, SH 9, and SH 7 at chainage 185.544 km, 196.605 knm and
216.816 km respectively. The respective angles of crossing are 38°, 750 and 830.
2.3.10   Under Passes
The under passes are two types a) Pedestrians crossing and b) Vehicular crossings Total
39 under passes are there out of which 7 are pedestrians crossings and 32 are vehicular
cum pedestrian crossings.
2.3.10.1  Pedestrian underpasses
Pedestrian crossings have been provided at the chainages 162 725, 199.525, 217.925,
220.125, 227.475, 229.800, and 238.275. The pedestrian crossings comprise of single unit
R.C.C. Box Cells (2.5m (clear hight) x 3m (clear span)) with 3.5 m. approach slab on
either side.
2.3.10.2  Vehicular cum Pedestrian Underpasses
The vehicular underpasses are 7.5 m wide and accommodate 5.5 m carriageway with
1000 mm footway on either side. The minimum clear height of the underpasses is 3
meters. The Vehicular Underpasses locations are given in Table 2.2 below:

2.4       Bridges, Culverts and Other Structures
2.41     Introduction
Quite a large number of bridges and culverts have been proposed all along the project
road. Out of these 4 nos. of ROBs, 3 nos. of state highway crossings and 8 no. of river
channel crossings are significant. In addition, there are 9 numbers of irrigation canal
crossings whose span varies from 8 Om to 24.0 m These crossings intersect the bypass at
angles varying from 25 to 90 degree. Table 2.3 Summaries the numbers of bridges and
culverts other than bridge over Ganga.
Table 2.3: Bridges and Culverts
SL No. Descrintion                       ABP-2       ABP-3     Total No.
1      Bridge over stream                4           4          8

2.42      Proposal for Ganga Bridge
One major bridge over river Ganga is planned. The Ganga Bridge is the subject of a
major hydrological/hydraulic investigation by the Indian Institute of Technology,
Roorkee. The estimated lengtlh and height of HFL were assessed and used as the basis of
the engineering design of this bridge. The bridge is designed in two units of 506 4 m each
with a total length of 1.03 km. It consists of 8 spans of 95m each and 4 spans of 63.2m
each. Three expansion joints are provided one in the middle and at one each pier. The
spans are built of precast segments of variable size and stresses in place. The piers are
built on well foundations deep bellow the scour level. The maximum flood level was set
at 98m, providing a minimum free board of 1.5m. The proposed bridge will be able to
take the maximum flood flow of 21,000m3/sec.
2.43      Bridges on Streams and Canals
There are 11 canals and 8 streams on which bridges have been proposed.
2.4.3.1   Canals
The canals fall at chainages 192 348, 196 590, 197.934, 203.505, 216.825, 216.930,
222.950, 223.405, 224.211, 235.00 and 235 431. The canals are of varying width from 8
meter to 24 m and the crossing angles vary in between 250 to 900 The lowest angle of 250
is encountered at chainage 235 431

2.43.2  Streams
The streams that are crossed by the bypass alignment are at chainages 162.262, 166.362
(Doab),  178.982,  181.125, 209.468  (Mansaita),  231.934, 233.016,  233.567.
Reconnaissance survey of all the streams was carried out, and bridges and culverts over
them have been proposed depending upon the discharge pattern. Due consideration has
been given not to disturb the natural drainage pattern of the stream.
2A4       Cunerts
In the total stretch of the bypass 102 no. of culverts have been proposed over the
irrigation canals, natural drains, nullahs, etc. with the objective to reinstate natural
drainage pattern and irrigation system.
2.45     Sidewalks
The bypass will not be accessible to pedestrian traffic, therefore, the pedestrian walkway
was eliminated on the bridges with spans 30m or less, and GA drawings were prepared
accordingly. AASHTO standards recommend constructing a full shoulder width on all
structures, for operation and safety reasons. This is essential for emergency stopping or
broken-down vehicles, which will use this as a refuge while fast moving traffic uses the
road.
2.46      Slopes and Protection
Slopes need to be provided at high embankment stretches and locations of bridges.
Provision of 2:1 (H:V) slopes has been made for high embankments. This slope ratio has
been arrived based on angle of repose of soil to ensure stability of slopes and
embankments. The detail of justification of slopes and slope protection measures and
covered in chapter - 8.
2.47  Toll Road Portion and Non Access Controlled Area
The bypass will be a toll road. The length of toll road portion is 78.05 km. The portion of
bypass along existing national highway-2 will not be access controlled rest portion of the
bypass will be access controlled.
2.5       Pavement
Full rigid pavement has been considered for main carriageway. However, service roads
will have flexible pavement.
2.6       Cross Section
Four lane divided carriageway has been proposed. Two-lane carriage way, on either side
of 12m wide median, with 3.5m width of each lane has been proposed. An unpaved
compacted earth surface of 11 m wide has been designed on either side of main
carriageway. A paved shoulder of 1.5 m width and an unpaved shoulder of 1.0 m width
also been proposed along the main carriage way. A service road of 5.5 m width in semi
2-8 8

urban stretches and 3.75 m width in rural stretches is planned on either side of median. At
present median width in the new alignment has been proposed as 12 m. The median space
will be utilized in coming years when there will be widening from 4 lane to 6 lanes.

2.8      Design Standard of Bypass
The bypass will be developed as an access-controlled facility with necessary parallel
service-road and fencing, grade separation for all cross roads with access to Bypass only
through Interchanges. The project also has a provision for ultimate widening of the
Bypass to a divided six lane one when needed in future considering the traffic growth.
Though the project stretch has not been designated as an Expressway, the operative
speeds will be higher, on the toll road portion, than other stretches of National Highway-2
(NH-2).
From above considerations the Design Designation for the Bypass may be taken as
follows:
Terrain Classification Plain (less than 10% cross slope)

2.9       Pavement Structure
Pavement along entire stretch of bypass will be rigid. The DPR consultants have carried a
cost benefit analysis for package III. In this analysis rigid pavement for main carriageway
of Allahabad Bypass (package IIIB) has been found to be most optimal. Service roads,
however, will have flexible pavement.
ProjectCost
Total project cost of Allahabad bypass is 12102 million INR. Out of this land acquisition
cost will be 878 million INR. Environmental mitigation and enhancement costs have been
summarized in chapter-8.
2.10      Overview of ImPacts of Project
The Direct and measurable benefits of the project arise from vehicle operating costs
(VoC) and travel time costs. The other direct benefits include improved access; to
markets, and health and educational facilities. Construction related jobs would be created
during implementation stage of the project There will be overall increase in safety and
comforts of road users
The project will provide a much-needed connectivity to vast rural areas in the Ganga
plains- The improved connectivity will undoubtedly bring prosperity to these
economically under developed regions.
Indirect benefits of project include the better business opportunities and increased
competitiveness within the area. Increased mobility of the population will mean more
integration of the region in economic, social and political terms as one entity.
There will also be adverse impacts of project relating to the strife in local population,
increased strain on environmentally sensitive receptors, consumption of (non renewable)
resources, changes and land use pattern, quality life, changes due to induced drainages
and increased pollution loads during constniction period. The adverse impacts will also

POLICY, LEGAL AND ADMINISTRATIVE
FRAME WORK
3.1   POLICY,LEGAL AND ADMINISTRATIVE FRAME WORK
The Allahabad Bypass Project has been initiated and is being carried out by the NHAI,
an authority which is a part of the Ministry of Road Transport and Highways
(MORT&H), Government of India. Though the primary responsibility of the project rests
with the NHAI, a brief dtscussion on the various institutions involved and their level of
responsibilities in the project implementation is presented in the following sections
3.2   Institutlonal Seting for the Project
The National Highways Authority of India (NHAI)
The mandate for the planning, design, implementation and maintenance of the 30,000km
National Highway network in India rests with the Ministry of Road Transport and'
Highway (MORT&H), Government of India. The MORT&H carries out its duttes of
maintaining these highways through the various state PWDs.
To plan and implement projects on a fast track and to expedite the improvements of the
important National Highways, the Government of India has established the National
Highways Authority of India (NHAI). Of the total national highways network in India,
the NHAI has been entrusted with the planning, development and management of
13,000km  forming part of the Golden Quadrilateral (connecting the four major
metropolitan cities in India, viz., Delhi, Kolkata, Mumbai and Chennai) and the north-
south and the East-West Corridors connecting Kashmir to Kanyakumari and Silchar to
Porbandar.
With an ambitious plan of the improvement of 13,000km national highway stretch by
2011, NHAI has initiated several highway development projects in various parts of the
country on a war footing, with funding from World Bank, Asian Development Bank etc.
apart from its own sources of funds.
The NHAI, through the Member (World Bank) will be responsible for the effective
implementation of the project activities. The Member is assisted by a General Manager
(WB) and the Project Directors of the various project packages. Separate NHAI units
known as Project Implementation Unit (PIU) headed by a Project Director have been
established for each of these packages. International consultants have been appointed for
carrying out the feasibility study and preparation of the detailed project reports and
designs for four-laning of the project packages.
in associatlon with m
4 070 210'1/ w                                    4w~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~vx



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2
Project Implementation Units
These are separate NHAI units established at the project locations for each of the project
packages, with a Project Director (PD) as its head. The Project Director is assisted on all
technical issues by two managers, which include a manager ( Technical and
Environment), and a manager ( R&R). These PlUs play a key role in implementation of
the project including the overall control of construction activities and implementation of
contracts. The various Project Directors, in turn report to the General Manager (WB),
who is responsible for the co-ordination of the project activities of the various project
packages.
The review of the preparation of the Environmental and Social assessments for the project
and ensuring the effective implementation of the Environmental Management Plan /
Resettlement Action Plan is carried out by the Environment and R&R Managers at the
PIU, in due consultation with the environmental and social development unit (ESDU) at
the Corporate Office
Environmental and Social Development Unit at the NHAI Corporate
NHAI, primarily being a road agency, till recent past did not have the capacity to address
the environmental/social issues related to the projects. To assess, advise and monitor the
environmental performance of the various projects being planned, designed and
implemented by the NHAI, an ESDU has been established at the Corporate Office of the
NHAI. The General Manager (Environment), who is assisted by a Deputy General
Manager (DGM) and two managers - one each for the Environmental and Resettlement
aspects, heads the ESDU.
The ESDU is responsible for the co-ordination of the environmental and social issues of
the various project packages and works in close interaction with the environmental
managers at the various PlUs.
3.3   Legal Frame Work
The Indian constitution makes environmental protection an explicit duty for every citizen
by the inclusion of the following in the fundamental duties of Indian citizens.
"It shall be duty of every citizen of India to protect and improve the environment
including forests, lakes, rivers, wild life, and to have compassion for living creatures".
In addition, Government of India has laid out various policy guidelines, acts and
regulations pertaining to sustenance of environment.
The legal framework for the project is summarized in following sections.

3.3.1  The Environment IProtectionl Act 1986 and the Environmental Impact Assessment Notification,
1994
The Environmental (Protection) Act, 1986 is the umbrella legislation providing for the
protection of environment in the country. This Act provides the Environment (Protection)
Rules, which were formulated in 1986. The Environmental Impact Assessment
Notification, 1994 and the various Amendment thereto have been notified under this act.
As per the Amendment, formal environmental clearance from the ministry is required for
highway widening and strengthening projects if they involve land acquisition of more
than 20m, on either side put together and/or cut across or pass through environmentally
sensitive areas as reserved forests, wildlife sanctuaries, biosphere reserves etc. Also, the
bypasses are to be treated as separate projects and require an EIA only if each one costs
more than INR 1000 million (Annexure 1). Under 'The Environment (Protection) Act',
1986, the developmental project requires clearances from the State Pollution Control
Board and Ministry of Environment and Forests, New Delhi. The procedure for obtaining
environmental clearance has been depicted in Figure 3.1.

3.3.2     The Water and Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Acts
The Water (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1974 resulted in the establishment
of the Central and State level Pollution Control Boards (CPCB and SPCB) whose
responsibilities include managing water quality and effluent standards, as well as
monitoring water quality, prosecuting offenders and issuing licenses for construction and
operation of certain facilities similarly. The Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution)
Act, 1981, empowers the SPCBs to enforce air quality standards set by the CPCB.
The project requires the obtaining of clearence from the State Pollution Control Board of
Uttar Pradesh pursuant to the Water (Prevention and Control of pollution) Act of 1974,
the cess Act of 1977 and Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act of 1981. The
UPPCB has already established a review panel and circulated the application for public
review and comment in each affected district. Public hearings have been completed in all
districts and NOC from the UPPCB is awaited.
3.3.3     The Forest [Conservation) Act 1980
The Forest (Conservation) Act, 1980 pertains to the cases of diversion of forest area for
non-forestry use. The process of obtaining forest clearance under this varies with the legal
status of the forestland to be diverted.
In case of Reserved Forest:
*   If the area of forests to be cleared or diverted exceeds 20ha (or, 10ha in hilly area)
then prior permission will be granted by MoEF, Gol, New Delhi
*   If the area of forest to be cleared or diverted is between 5 to 20ha, then the case would
be put to the state advtsory committee for constderation. The committee after studying
the case will recommend to MoEF, Gol for formal approval.
*   If the area of forest to be cleared or diverted is below or equal to 5ha, than the MoEF
regional office is empowered to give the approval.
*   If the area to be clear-felled has a forest density of more than 40%, permission to
undertake any work is needed from the Central Government, irrespective of the area
to be cleared.
In case of Protected Forest
MoEF regional office is empowered to accord Forest clearance irrespective of area to be
cleared.
The above clearance procedure has been shown in the form of flow chart in Figure 3.2. In
the present case forest clearance will be necessary, for felling of trees from portion of
bypass falling along existing NH-2.

3.3.4  The Wild Life (Protection) Act 1972
The Wildlife (Protection) Act, 1972 has allowed the government to establish a number of
National Parks and Sanctuaries over the past 25 years, to protect and conserve the flora
and fauna of the state. The act will not be applicable as the proposed alignment does not
pass through any National Park or Sanctuary.
3.3.5  The Motor Vehicles Act 1988
In 1988, the Indian Motor Vehicles Act empowered the State Transport Authority
(usually the Road Transport Office) to enforce standards for vehicular pollution and
prevention control. The authority also checks emission standards of registered vehicles,
collects road taxes, and issues licenses. In August 1997, the Pollution Under Control
Certificate (PUC) programme was launched in an attempt to crackdown on the vehicular
emissions in the States. Since this act is applicable for all states, this will be applicable for
this project also.
3.3.6  The Ancient Monuments and Archaeological Sites and Remains Act 1958
According to this Act, area within the radii of 100m and 300m from the "protected
property" are designated as "protected area" and "controlled area" respectively. No
development activity (including building, mining, excavating, blasting) is permitted in the
''protected area" and development activities likely to damage the protected property are
not permitted in the "controlled area" without prior permission of the Archaeological
Survey of India (ASI) if the site/remains/ monuments are protected by ASI or the State D
are protected by the State. The proposed alignment is not close to any protected property
and therefore, this act will not be applicable for the project. However, there may be
chance find of Artefacts or coins, structures, fabrics or any other archaeological
relics during construction phase. In such situation this act will be applicable.
3.3.7  State Level Legislaton and Other Acts
The Forest (Conservation) Act, 1980 has been amended to include roadside plantations as
protected forest. Under this, the NHAI has to obtain clearance from the Forest
Department for cutting the trees.
Consent of the SPCB will be required for setting up hot-mix plants, batching plants, etc.,
under the Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act of 1981 and the Water
(Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act of 1974. Clearance from the State Department
of Mining is required for establishing new quarries. Clearance from the State Ground
Water Boards/Authorities is required for establishment of new tube-wells/bore-holes, etc.
Further sand will be mined from Ganga for blending in earth to meet the earth work
requirements. The clearance is required from the river authorities.
In addition, with respect to hygiene and health, during the construction period, the
provisions as laid down in the Factories Act, 1948 and the Building and Other
Construction Workers (Regulation of Employment and Conditions of Service) Act, 1996
would apply.

With limited possibility, the provisions of the Hazardous Wastes (Management and
Handling) Rules, 1989 and the Chemical Accidents (Emergency Planning, Preparedness
and Response) Rules, 1996 would also apply during the construction and the operation
periods.

3A    InstituUonal Seting In the Environmental Context
The environmental regulations, legislations, policy guidelines and control that may
impact this project, are the responsibility of a variety of government agencies. In all, as
discussed in the subsequent sections, the following agencies play important roles in this
project.
3A1   Minist of Environment and Forests IMOEFI
The primary responsibility for administration and implementation of the Government of
India's policy with respect to environmental management, conservation, ecologically
sustainable development and pollution control rests with the Ministry of Environment and
Forests (MoEF). Established in 1983, MoEF is the agency primarily responsible for the
review and approval of EIA pursuant to GOI legislation.
3A2   MOEF Regional Offices
The Ministry of Environment and Forests has set up regional offices, in the four regions
of the country. The regional office for the present project is located at Lucknow. This
office is responsible for collection and furnishing of state information relating to EIA of
projects, pollution control measures, status of compliance of various conditions in
projects cleared by MoEF, legal and enforcement measures and environmental protection
in special conservation areas such as wetlands, and other biological reserves.
3A3   Central Pollution Control Board lCPCBI
CPCB is a statutory authority attached to the MoEF and located in New Delhi. The main
responsibilities of CPCB include interalia the following
* Plan and implement water and air pollution monitoring programs
* Advise the Central Government on water and air pollution monitoring programs
*  Set air and water standards, and
*  Co-ordinate with the State Pollution Control Boards.
3AA   State Pollution Control Boards ISPCBI
The Uttar Pradesh Pollution Control Board (UPPCB) will be the government agency
responsible for ensuring the compliance with the relevant standards related to discharge
of pollutant into the environment. The following activities of the UPPCB will be relevant
to the project.
* Planning and executing state level air and water quality initiatives
* Advising state government on air, water and industry issues
* Establishing standards based on National Minimum standards
* Enforcing monitoring of all activities within the state under The Air Act, The Water
Act and the Cess Act etc., and

3A5    Deparinents of Forests [DonF of Uttar Prasesh
The department of forests, Govt. of Uttar Pradesh is responsible for the management and
administration of forest resource in the state. The road side plantations in the state of
Uttar Pradesh have been designated as protected forests. Only along existing National
Highway-2 that is a declared protected forest, permission from state forest department
has to be taken for cutting of trees as per the Forest (Conservation) Act, 1980.
Compensatory afforestation work will be undertaken in lieu of tree cutting from the
designated protected forests area on the existing NH 2 section. This being a new
alignment does not fall under category of protected forests hence pennission to cut
private trees have to be obtained from forest department Contractors will implement the
plantation in median with technical assistance from state forest department.
3.5    Environmental Clearance Requirements
3.5.1  GOI Reunirements
The primary responsibility for administration and implementation of the Govt. Policy
with respect to conservation, ecologically sustainable development and pollution control
rests with the MOEF. The MOEF is responsible to enforce the regulations established
pursuant to the National Conservation Strategy, National Forest Policy, the Policy for
Abatement of Pollution (1992) and the Indian Environmental (Protection) Act 1986.
The Environmental Impact Assessment Notification, 1994 identified highways (item 21
of Schedule-1) as one of the projects requiring prior clearance from the MoEF. Therefore,
the environmental impact assessment (EIA) is a statutory requirement for obtaining
clearance.
In April 1997, a circular was issued by MoEF amending Schedule-I of the EIA
Notification, 1994 which lists projects requiring Environmental Clearance.
'...Environmental Clearance from the MOEF is not required for Highway project relating to improvement
work including widening and strengthening of roads with marginal land acquisition along the existing
alignments provided the highways do not pass through ecologically sensitive areas such as National
Parks, Sanctuaries. Tiger Reserve, Reserve Forests etc.'
Source: Gazette Notificationt, Govenmnaent of India, dated April 19, 97
'...As per the provisions of the EIA Notification of 27th January 94 and as amended on 10th April, 97,
environmental clearance is required for highway projects except projects relating to improvement work
including widening and strengthening of roads with marginal land acquisition along the existing
alignments provided they do not pass through ecologically sensitive areas such as national parks,
sanctuaries, tiger reserves, reserve forests. It is hereby clarified that marginal land acquisition means land
acquisition not exceeding a total width of 20 meters on either side of the existing alignment put together.
Further, it is also clarified that bypasses would be treated as stand-alone projects and would require
environmental clearance only if the cost of the projects exceed Rs.100 crores each'.
Source.]. MOEF Circular No. 2]01Z126-99-/A-M dated 13 October ]999.
2. MOEF Circular No. Z-12013/4/89-IA. I (Part ) dated 13 Junze,2002

classification system of the projects such as 'Category A', 'Category B' and 'Category
C'. A project designated as 'Category A', requires a full environmental assessment (EA),
'Category B' projects require a lesser level of environmental investigation where as

environmental and social impacts and therefore, detailed environmental and social
assessment is mandatary for this project. The independent reviewers have verified
conformance of the World Bank Operational Policies in the project and have consolidated
the EA and EMP accordingly. The emphasis of the World Bank is on integration of the
mitigation measures into the project design and mainstreaming environment in all stages
of planning, implementation and operation.

c                                     Approach & Methodology
4.1  Methodology
The environmental assessment in this project employed an iterative approach in
which potential environmental issues have been examined in successive levels of
detail and specificity at each step in the process. This chapter presents the
methodology adopted for the EIA preparation for the Allahabad Bypass project for
the mainstreaming of the environmental considerations in this project.
4.2  Environmental considerations Incorporated in the feasibility study
The environmental assessment for Allahabad Bypass project began with the
adoption of an environmental and social screening procedure during the feasibility
stage. The purpose of the screening was to identify at the outset, key environmental
and social parameters likely to be impacted by the project. The information
gathered on these issues during the screening was subjected to detail assessment
during EIA study. The features studied included settlement facilities, land use,
drainage pattern of the area, flora, fauna, ecosystems within ROW of the alignment,
river crossing, cross roads, etc. The study of the above helped in enumerating areas
of concern and identifying critical issues.
43   Envronmental Impact Assessment
The environmental impact assessment was undertaken simultaneously with detailed
design of the bypass. The important findings of the assessment gave important
feedback to the design team, especially in terms of the sensitive receptors, utility
/facilities to be impacted. It helped modify the designs at locations where impacts
had to be avoided and incorporate mitigation measures wherever the impacts were
unavoidable due to other constraints. The stepwise methodology adopted for the
EIA is as follows:
4.3.1 Scoping
The Terms of Reference of the DPR Consultants, the statutory requirements for the
area of influence required by the Ministry of Environment and Forests, and
consultations with experts determined the scope of the assessment for this study. In
addition the screening study undertaken identified valued ecosystem components
(VECs) in the project. The valued ecosystem components identified were major
rivers ecosystems (Ganga, Mansaita and Doab), plantation, orchards, community
water sources, ponds, etc.
InassoelatlonwU-1
In association with



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2
4.3.2 Reconnaissance Surveys
The study team of DPR consultants visited the selected alignment and information
on each kilometres was collected. Detailed strip mapping carried out as part of the
detailed design data collection also provided valuable information regarding area
adjacent to the alignment.
4.33 Assembly and Analysis of Data
The data from surveys was fed to customised spreadsheets for ease of analysis.
Supplementary information was collected from Survey of India toposheets, census
handbooks and other government publications as well as reports prepared for other
projects in Uttar Pradesh. Standard statistical techniques were used for analysis of
the socio-economic data, the tree count, etc. Qualitative analysis was done for more
descriptive data.
43A  Documentation of Baseline Conditions
The documentation of the baseline conditions was completed for a 7.5 km wide
strip on both sides of the bypass alignment as Project Influence Area (PIA) as per
the MoEF guidelines. Primary surveys were carried out for determination of
ambient air quality, water quality, soil quality at various locations 'along the
proposed alignment and noise levels were also measured at different locations to
have an idea of prevailing noise levels in the area. A detailed chainage tree count at
km interval in the ROW of bypass was taken.
4.3.5 Assessment of alternatives
Alternatives were continuously assessed throughout the process. DPR consultants
did a detailed study on selection of alternatives during feasibility report preparation.
A more formal assessment was also undertaken as a part of the environmental
assessment process, including the assessment of the "No Action" alternative as is
customarily included as a part of the formal assessment methodologies to ensure
that it has been given proper consideration.
4.3.6 Assessment of Potential Impacts
Potential and significant impacts were identified on the basis of analytical review of
baseline data; review of land uses and environmental factors; analytical review of
the socio-economic conditions within the PIA; and review of assessment of
potential impacts as identified by previous highway projects.
43.3 Integration of Environmental Impacts In the Design Process: "Mainstreaming the Environmental
Componenf'
The design and decision-making process integrated environmental, resettlement and
rehabilitation issues and prompted the early identification of appropriate actions.
Such actions included, for example, shifts in alignments based on awareness of the
locations of settlements cultural resources, and biological resources along the
bypass alignment.

4.3.8 Identified Mitgation and Enhancement Measures
Positive actions, to not only avoid adverse impacts, but to capitalize on
opportunities to correct environmental degradation or improve environmental
conditions were determined.
43.9 Communiy Consultations
Extensive consultations were held at various stages of the project. Chapter six of
this report details out the methods, approaches and outcomes of the public
consultations held. The issues raised by the communities and the various
stakeholders were incorporated in the design and construction/operation plan of the
project highway. The consultations held are more in line with World Bank
requirements. The public hearing has also been carried out as per requirements of
MoEF.
43.10 Preparation of the Environmental Management Plans
Environmental management plans have been prepared for each construction
package separately as part of the Environmental Assessment. Responsibilities have
been assigned for the various actions identified to limit the adverse impacts of the
project and budget allocations have been made for the funds required for mitigation
as well as enhancement measures. The environmental management plans have been
prepared for three phases namely Pre Construction, construction and Operation
(Post Construction) phase.
44   Finalising the environmental assessment
Environmental assessment has been completed based on input received from World
Bank Mission during the DPR preparation and experience of reviewers on similar
other projects. In addition, the field visits of reviewers provided valuable inputs into
the completion of the EA. The baseline environmental setting, the potential impacts
and the plausible mitigation measures have been supplemented based on the field
visits.
441 Completng the Baseline
The baseline has been completed by measuring air, water and soil quality and noise
level in the area of influence of project. The information was evaluated using the
same VECs, which were identified during the environmental screening. Since
baseline data was not on expected pattern therefore reviewers have collected data
during December month to revalidate the baseline. The detailed methodology of
baseline data collection has been summarised in Annex 2.

Impact assessment was carried out for the baseline environmental quality, which
has been established along the alignment. In addition, the updated baseline and
other information collected about sensitive receptors during the subsequent site

demarcation of responsibilities of the various institutions responsible. Monitoring
systems have been established to ensure ease of follow-up activities.

Environmental Scenario
5.0   ExIsting Environmental Scenario
This chapter assesses the nature, type and dimensions of the study area and describes the
relevant physical and biological environmental components along the bypass alignment.
The database on the environmental components relevant to decisions about project
location, design and operation have been assembled from various secondary sources and
primary surveys conducted by DPR consultants. Further, Independent consultants have
been undertaken field visits, collected limited secondary and primary data to supplement
the data gap, impart consistency and comprehensiveness in data base.
5.1   Meteorologlcal baseline
The study of meteorological and micro meteorological parameters is significant in a road
project as these parameters will be guiding factors for dispersion of pollutants released
into the atmosphere.
5.1.1  Climate
The climate of Uttar Pradesh is mainly sub-tropical with the hilly region of Terai at north
having more temperate characteristics. The Allahabad bypass project of NHAI is located
in the humid sub-tropical region with marked monsoon effects. The summers are
generally hot and dry, while cold weather prevails in winters. Due to large variations in
temperature and rainfall, the climate has a characteristic seasonality. The climate is
distinguished by three distinct seasons:
Hot Summer (from April to June),
Warmn humid Rainy season (from July to September), and
*     Cold Winter (from November to February).
October and March constitute the transitional months between rainy, winter and summer
seasons respectively.
5.1.2  Temperature
The temperature in the region varies from extreme high temperatures upto 47°C in
summer to as low as 4°C in winter. The annual average temperature is around 27°C as per
meteorological data of Allahabad IMD observatory for the 1951-1980 duration.
5.1.3  Wind
The entire stretch of Allahabad bypass is in plain agriculture area and the area of project
route experiences light to moderate winds. A hot wind, locally known as 'loo', blow from
the west during the summer. However, the wind speeds are quite low though there is the
5 -1
In association with



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2
occasional storm. The average wind speed is in the range of 4-5 km/h. The wind rose
diagrams for Allahabad IMD observatory are shown in Figure 5.1 for winter season. It is
clear from this figure that dominant direction from which wind blows is NW.
5.1A   Rainfall
The rainfall in the Allahabad region is almost exclusively due to monsoon. The yearly
average rainfall in the project area is about 1000 mm more than 90% of which occurs
during the rainy season. August is the month of highest rainfall with about 31 cm rain
while April and November are the driest months withfrainfall less than <0.5 cm.
5.1.5  Relative Humidity
The region on an average experiences relative humidity of 64%. The air is very humid
during rainy season when relative humidity reaches upto 80% (as per data of Allahabad
IMD observatory.
5.2    Natural and Biohvyslcal Environment
Accurate determination of baseline conditions of natural and biophysical environmental
components along project route is vital for robust impact assessment. The components of
the environment for which the information has been collected are described in the
following subsections.
5.2.1 Air
Degradation of ambient air quality is the most commonly identified adverse impact on the
natural and bio - physical environment along the new alignment of road projects.

SPM  can possibly be attributed to the soil, which is alluvial and therefore, has a
propensity to get suspended in air. In addition, in settlements, other anthropogenic
activities too may generate a lot of suspended particulates, which results in high
concentrations recorded during the survey.
5.2.2  Water: Hydrology and Drainage
Water is relatively abundant in the study area, mainly due to the presence of the snow-fed
perennial river Ganga. However, the concern with water is as much about quality as it is
about quantity available. New road alignments as in case of Allahabad Bypass can
significantly alter the hydrological setting of the project area by acting as an impediment
to the natural drainage system of the region. They can also alter ground water recharge
patterns by creating impervious surfaces as part of the pavement as well as by diverting
flow away from groundwater recharge areas.
Surface Water
The Allahabad bypass alignment is located in water rich area due to presence of river
Ganga in project influence area. The general drainage along the project routes is in the
direction of the Ganga so that the riv'ers and other streams north of Ganga flow towards
south and east while those south of the Ganga flow north and east. Along the project
stretch a number of different water sources are found. These can be broadly classified into
two categories
-     Flowing water resources, viz rivers, streams
-     Stagnant water resources viz ponds, ditches, etc.
The proposed bypass alignment is crossing numerous streams, drains, canals and three
rivers namely Ganga, Doab and Mansaita. The region under study is a part of river Ganga
plain, physiographically the entire Allahabad bypass package is located in:
-     the lower Ganga-Yamuna Doab (km 158 - km 163.267); and
-     the trans Ganga region (km 164.280 - km 242.708)
The slope of the area is from northwest (km 158, Kokhraj) to south east (km 245, Handia)
with an average gradient of 16 cm/km. Thus, the project area of the proposed bypass is
almost a flat terrain.
The area as a whole is a depositional terrain i.e. the alluvial plain of Ganga river. The
river Ganga, other rivers and nullahs namely Doab, Mansaita, Bairagya and Basana are
not meandering in nature. The active channel and their flood plains are confined within
fixed lateral stretches. The independent reviewers have verified during field visits that
there is no record of frequent change of river channels flow directions in this area.
The alignment of proposed Allahabad bypass after crossing river Ganga at Dhimi runs
parallel to north bank upto a distance of 6 km. The minimum distance of bypass
alignment has been maintained 500 m from the flood plain. The DPR consultants
mentioned about the presence of abandoned channel and Oxbow lake in project area
though they did not mention exact location. During the field visits Independent review
consultants verified that no oxbow lake and abandoned channel of Ganga or any other
river are present on the proposed bypass alignment
5 -4
in association with



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2

number of ponds also lie either adjacent to proposed alignment or within the RoW of the

5.2.3  Land Environment
Geology
The region through which the highway passes is geo-morphologically a part of the Ganga
plain. This has been formed during the Pleistocene period by filling of the Indo-Ganga
system. It has 400-1500m deep alluvial cover. The base in the project area is quite deep,
probably more than 1000-4000m below the ground.
The older alluvium is dark colored locally called 'Bhanger'. Bhanger is rich in impure
Calcium Carbonate commonly known as 'Kankars'. The Kankars are of different shapes
and sizes varying from grains to big lumps. Carbonate concentration is evident in the
Bhanger alluvium at many places. The age of the "Bhanger" alluvium is middle to upper
Pleistocene.
The newer alluvium locally called "Khadar" is light colored and poor in calcium
carbonate concentrations. It contains beds of sand and gravel as well as peat beds. It is
merged by mild gradations into the recent or deltaic alluvia and its age is upper
Pleistocene to Recent.
The proposed bypass alignment is passing through Seismic Zone II as indicated in IRC-6-
1996 clause 222.3 and appendix 5 of IRC 78-1979. Basically from BIS zoning map
(IS:1893) the Allahabad region indicates a low key of potential earthquake with Zone II
having a base horizontal seismic coefficient of 0.02.
Geological investigations at Ganga bridge site have indicated chances of occurrence of
liquefaction phenomenon due to loose sandy deposits in top 12 to 15 m soil strata.
Investigations at Ganga bridge site have also indicated artesian layers in two bore holes
out of seven. In the high embankment stretches at other locations no such conditions have
been observed.
Soils
The Allahabad bypass alignment is largely located in Ganga flatland. The area as a whole
is the depositional terrain i.e. the alluvial plain of Ganga river. In general the soil along
the road alignment is alluvial, pale brown to whitish in color and loam to sandy loam in
texture (sand 40-45%, silt 28%, and clay 27%). The soils are neutral to moderately
alkaline with accumulation of calcium in the form of lime at varying depths and sizes.
The chemical analysis of the soil for the various parameters including electrical
In association with                 J

conductivity, organic carbon, phosphate, potassium and nitrogen has been provided in
Table 5.9. The independent reviewers also collected few soil samples durng air and
water sampling to check the trend of soil characteristics in the region and similar results
were obtained from these samples.

Landuse
Proposed bypass alignment is passing through predominantly rural agricultural land, with
scattered community water bodies, orchards, villages etc. It is not affecting any major
human settlements. Only 97 village households are getting affected.
Total land required for the proposed bypass is 781 Ha. On the whole 84% is cultivable.
Of the total land about 80% is irrigated agricultural land, 10% non-irrigated agricultural
land, about 7-8% fallow land, and balance 2-3% water bodies (both flowing and
stagnant). The land use pattern does not include any forest area. The land use pattern of
project influence area has been shown in Figure 5.4.
Soil Erosion
The bypass alignment is crossing three rivers, some drains, streams and canals. The banks
of all canal are fairly stabilized as these are man made. Most of the drains and streams are
none perennial and have small catchment areas. No significant soil erosion at the bank of
these drains and canals has been observed during the field visits of reviewers as extensive
agriculture activities has been seen in project influence area.
The three major rivers namely the Ganga, Mansaita and Doab are being crossed by the
bypass alignment. The left bank of Ganga is very stable. The active channels of all rivers
are not meandering in the project influence area There is no record of frequent change in
5-10
44#1p#~~~~~w ~In association with0



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2                                                00   - I 
the direction of active channel of these rivers. Soil erosion problems in the region have
also not been highlighted at any of public consultations during EIA study.
Wind Erosion
The wind erosion is not a prominent factor for soil erosion in project influence area. The
DPR consultants had identified Rahimpatty village prone to soil erosion. But visit to
Rahimpatty village (Photo Plate 2) and discussion with locals indicates no occurrence of
such phenomena.

Quarries & Crushers
Road construction activities are closely linked with quarry sites around the work sites.
The construction materials including stones and sand are needed in large quantities for the
pavement construction.
The UPPWD for its entire network has approved several quarries for obtaining sand and
aggregates. These same existing quarries that are already in operation with the required
environmental clearances have been recommended for this project also, and no new
quarries have been proposed. The following quarries (Table 5.10) were identified in Uttar
Pradesh. The location of these quarries has been shown in Figure 5.7.

Borrow Pits
The soils to be used, as sub-grade, select sub-grade and shoulder materials need to be
hauled from designated borrow areas. Similar to the identification of suitable quarries,
suitable borrow areas for supply of soil to the new road formation were also identified.
Based on the total requirement and availability of each soil type, estimates of soil quantity
to be obtained from each of the borrow areas were worked out in accordance with the
National Standards, recommended by the Indian Roads Congress (IRC)l.
In the selection of the borrow areas, care was taken to ensure that:
sufficient quantity of suitable soil is available from the borrow pit
the borrow areas are as close to the proposed alignment road as far as
possible
the loss of productive and fertile agricultural soil is minimum and
*     there is minimum loss of vegetation.
In order to keep the lead manageable, a maximum distance of 100 - 600 m was assumed
for all routes. Details of borrow pits identified along the project route are shown in
Figures 5.8-5.9 and Table 5.11. The design team has estimated that the material available
from these pits will not be enough for the construction works. In order to meet additional
requirements of earth works, blending of sand is proposed. The sand for this purpose will
be mined from Ganga riverbed.

5.2.4  Noise
Noise attributed to roads depends on factors such as traffic intensity, the type and
condition of the vehicles plying on the road, acceleration/deceleration/gear changes by the
vehicles depending on the level of congestion and smoothness of road surface (IRC: 104-
1988). Excessively high noise levels are a concern for sensitive receptors, i.e., hospitals,
educational institutions, etc.
National Noise Standards
The Central Pollution Control Board has specified ambient noise levels for dtfferent land
uses for day and night times Importance was given to the timing of exposure and areas
designated as sensitive. Table 5.12 gives the noise standards set.
Table 5.12
National ambient noise level standards
Area       Cate              _   Limits in Decibels (dB A)

|...] Industrnal - - - -75   ----         70

c    I Residental _         55      I           45
D    I Silence Zones        50                  40
Note (I) Daytime: 6 AM to 9 P M, Night-time 9 PM to 6 AM,
(2)  Silence zone is an area up to 100 m around premises as hospitals, educational institutions and couns
Source Central Pollution Control Board, New Delhi
Selection of Sampling Locations
Locations for noise monitoring along the route were identified based on the criteria same
as those used for air monitoring but the relative importance of each criteria carries a
weightage in arriving at the final set of locations. In case of noise monitoring locations,
sensitive receptors such as schools, hospitals and religious places gains more importance
due to ill effects of noise. In all, 10 locations were selected for monitoring of noise levels
to cover all types of sensitive receptors and all the possible routes.
Results and Analysis of Noise Monitoring
The noise level measurements carried out by the DPR consultants are given in Table
5.13. It is clear from this table that results are just instantaneous values during day and
night and not logarithmic average of day and hourly values. Further these values are
exceptionally very high and not as expected in the region. It was, therefore, independent
reviewers were decided to measure the noise levels again.
It has been found from the independent reviewers noise monitoring results that the noise
levels at start and end point of bypass exceed the prescribed limits due to heavy traffic.
But at rural locations the day and night 'Leq' values are well within the stipulated limits
of CPCB for residential areas. The night time noise levels were lower than the
corresponding daytime measures. A variation of more than 5 dB (A) to 10 dB (A) was
observed at the monitoring locations between the day and nighttime noise levels.

5.2.5  Terrestrial Ecology, Vegetation and Habitats
Vegetation and Habitats
The study area is predominantly cultivated land interrupted by scattered human
settlements with cluster of thatched houses, orchards and common native trees present
here and there near to the houses, along the village roads, on the bank of the streams/
canals and inside the agricultural land. Narrow strip of grasses are encountered at the
ripanan zone of the river Ganga/ other flowing water bodies.
Source of Information
Beside the field survey, secondary data on general vegetation have been collected from
1.  Botanical Survey of India (BSI), Allahabad university  and divisional forest office
of Allahabad
2.   The Toposheets of Survey of India

Vegetation
Crops in the fields are the predominant features of the study area (Photo Plate-3).
g~~~~jj ~  ~     / .
| Photo Plate-3 |
Besides the crops other natural vegetation on the project influenced area of proposed
Allahabad bypass are common trees i.e., Mango, Jack fruits, Jamun, Guava, Shisham,
Neem, Amla, Mohua, Barh, Baher, Gular, Babool, Eucalyptus etc. List of the plant
species found along the alignment have been presented in Annexure 5.
A total of 3298 numbers of trees have been identified to be cut from the RoW of the
proposed bypass alignment. This includes about 1046 fruit bearing trees from the thirty
affected orchards that covers around 3 ha of area (Annexure 6).
The affected trees can be categorized into three types:
- Trees from protected forest-where trees are planted by the forest department are
observed in the initial and end part of the bypass from km 158-km 160 and km
241.6-km 242.708.
- Private trees, which are dotted through out the bypass alignment
- Fruit bearing trees from affected orchards.
The number of trees falling on the existing NH-2 has been separately presented in the
Annexure 7.

General Habitat of Wild Animals
The Allahabad bypass is passing through very fertile cultivated land. At the start (at
Kokhraj, Chainage 158 km) bypass runs parallel to the existing NH-2 for about 2 kms. In
this stretch bypass corridor is passing through roadside protected forests and partiality
through the adjacent agriculture field. Then it turns left towards Ganga. After about 6 km
passing through cropland it crosses river Ganga. A 100-200 m wide strip of tall grasses
dominated by Saccharum species is found on both the banks of the river Ganga. Narrow
strip of tall grass is also found on the banks of streams, irrigation canals, both the sides of
village roads, out skirt of the villages or orchards and also in some isolated patches within
the crop fields. These grass patches are dominated by Saccharumn spontaneum, S.
benghalensis and Vetiveria zizanoides commonly known as Munj and Khas. These
grasses protect the soil from erosion. Grasses like Desmochya bipinnata, Cenchrus
ciliaris, Aristida hIysterix are commonly encountered in eroded and dry sites.
After crossing Ganga, (at chainage 163.4 to 164.350 km) (Photo Plate-4) bypass
alignment runs parallel to the left bank of the river upto a distance about six kilometers.
Here it crosses riparian grass patches, crop fields, natural drains, small streams, and
irrigation channels. Rest of the bypass traverses mainly through productive agricultural
field till it joins with NH-2 at Handia (at chainage 245 km on existing NH-2). The
landscape is occasionally interrupted by patches of orchards (Mango, Guava, Mohua,
Jack fruits) streams, canals, village ponds and some places human settlements.
Photo Plate - 4
No wetland is present in any part of the alignment. The alignment at Saunra village
touches tip of the Bhaunra Tal (pond) which is a "C' shaped water body. This part of
the water body has already become shallow with the passage of time and infested with
aquatic weeds like water hyacinth and Ipomea sp. (Photo Plate 5). Moreover,
cultivated lands surround this, which is gradually encroaching the edge of this Tal.
During non-monsoon season this part of the water body becomes dry.

Photo Plate-5
There are some natural depressions in which monsoon water accumulate and persists
for four-five months (Photo Plate-6). These water bodies gradually covered by
Cyprus (sedges) (Annexure 5) like vegetation. In summer these lands dry up and
become grazing ground for cattle.
Photo Plate -6:Seasonal water body
(at Gauharpur, 200m away from alignment)
The agricultural lands through which bypass alignment passes are highly productive and
give four crops annually. Common crop from these lands are Wheat, Bajra, Mustard,
Arhar, Peas, and vegetables like, Potato, Guards, Chilli, Tomato, Sweet Potato,
Pumpkins, Watermelons etc.
Productivity varies from crop to crop. Average production from one acre of land is in case
of Wheat, 18-20 quintals; Rice, about 25 quintals; Potato, about 150 quintals; and
Mustard, about 8 quintals. Farmers earn more from vegetables. As revealed during public
consultation average income from vegetables per acre of land per season is around one
lakh rupees.

The project-influenced area does not have any forest land except the road side protected
forest at the beginning and end of the bypass which is planted and maintained by the
forest department. No endangered species of vegetation have been recorded within the
study area.
Terrestrial Ecology -Wild LIfe
The entire length of Allahabad bypass falls mainly under the administrative control of
Kausambi and Allahabad forest divisions. Around 6 kms stretch between Kokhraj to
Sakhara belongs to Kausambi forest division and on the other side of the river Ganga,
from Dhirni to Handia, is under Allahabad forest division. No national park, sanctuary,
wild life reserves or reserved forests are present in near vicinity. Location of nearest
sanctuary/ natural park/wild life reserves along with the distance from the proposed
Allahabad bypass alignment has been presented in Figure 5.10.
The nearest wildlife sanctuary (Chitrakoot) is beyond 60 kms from the bypass alignment.

Inhabitance of Wild Animals
The wild fauna are negligible within the study area. Common native fauna like Neelgai,
Hyaena, Jackal, Rabbit, Porcupine etc. are rarely encountered in village out skirts and in
the bank of rivers/streams. Whatever wild fauna are present in this area, their numbers are
5-26
In association with-



Consolidated Environmental Impact Assessment Report for
Allahabad Bypass of NH-2                                                O,
very less particularly in the area around the proposed bypass alignment. Increasing human
interference like cultivation, use of pesticides, destruction of habitat i.e., clearing the
grass covers and indiscriminate poaching responsible for reduction in number of wild
fauna in this area.
Incidence of sighting of wild animals (Jackal, porcupine, rabbit etc.) is more near to the
banks of river Ganga where the grass patches are present (Photo Plate 7). There is no
permanent habitat of wild animals within the project-influenced area.
As per the information collected from different sources, wild animals encountered within
the study area particularly at the near vicinity of the proposed bypass alignment include
35 varieties of animals including birds and reptiles, among which 10 varieties are
mammals, 6 are reptiles and rest are birds (19 types) (Table 5.14). Among the ten
mammals, none are endangered/vulnerable/rare species as per Indian Wildlife
(Protection) Act, 1972 (amended in 1980) or as per the list of International Union for
Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources (IUCN). One scheduled I [as per Indian
Wildlife (Protection) Act, 1972] species of birds Pavo cristatus (Peacock), the national
bird of India is also found in this area (Photo Plate 8).

The animals like Antelope cervicarpra (Antelop), Sus scrofa (wild boar), Gaviallis

cross-checked with field workers of the respective forest offices and local people during
their field visits and confirmed the non existence of such fauna within the study area.
Further, independent reviewers also confirmed that no migratory route of any wild animal
is present in the Allahabad bypass influenced area.
